	</label>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1239400231" /><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Internet_protocol_suite" title="Template:Internet protocol suite"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Internet_protocol_suite" title="Template talk:Internet protocol suite"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Internet_protocol_suite" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Internet protocol suite"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>HTTP</b> (<b>Hypertext Transfer Protocol</b>) is an <a href="/wiki/Application_layer" title="Application layer">application layer</a> protocol in the <a href="/wiki/Internet_protocol_suite" title="Internet protocol suite">Internet protocol suite</a> for distributed, collaborative, <a href="/wiki/Hypermedia" title="Hypermedia">hypermedia</a> information systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Internet&#95;Engineering&#95;Task&#95;Force-2022&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Internet_Engineering_Task_Force-2022-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> HTTP is the foundation of data communication for the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">World Wide Web</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Hypertext" title="Hypertext">hypertext</a> documents include <a href="/wiki/Hyperlink" title="Hyperlink">hyperlinks</a> to other resources that the user can easily access, for example by a <a href="/wiki/Computer_mouse" title="Computer mouse">mouse</a> click or by tapping the screen in a <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browser</a>.
</p><p>HTTP is a <a href="/wiki/Request%E2%80%93response" title="Request–response">request–response</a> protocol in the <a href="/wiki/Client%E2%80%93server_model" title="Client–server model">client–server model</a>. A transaction starts with a client submitting a request to the server, the server attempts to satisfy the request and returns a response to the client that describes the disposition of the request and optionally contains a requested resource such as an <a href="/wiki/HTML" title="HTML">HTML</a> document or other content.
</p><p>In a common scenario, a <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browser</a> acts as the <a href="/wiki/Client_(computing)" title="Client (computing)">client</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Web_server" title="Web server">web server</a>, <a href="/wiki/Host_(network)" title="Host (network)">hosting</a> one or more <a href="/wiki/Website" title="Website">websites</a>, is the <a href="/wiki/Server_(computing)" title="Server (computing)">server</a>. A web browser is an example of a <a href="/wiki/User_agent" title="User agent">user agent</a> (UA). Other types of user agent include the indexing software used by search providers (<a href="/wiki/Web_crawler" title="Web crawler">web crawlers</a>), <a href="/wiki/Voice_browser" title="Voice browser">voice browsers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">mobile apps</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a> that accesses, consumes, or displays web content.
</p><p>HTTP is designed to permit intermediate network elements to improve or enable communications between clients and servers. High-traffic websites often benefit from <a href="/wiki/Web_cache" title="Web cache">web cache</a> servers that deliver content on behalf of <a href="/wiki/Upstream_server" title="Upstream server">upstream servers</a> to improve response time. Web browsers cache previously accessed web resources and reuse them, whenever possible, to reduce network traffic. HTTP <a href="/wiki/Proxy_server" title="Proxy server">proxy servers</a> at <a href="/wiki/Private_network" title="Private network">private network</a> boundaries can facilitate communication for clients without a globally routable address, by relaying messages with external servers.
</p><p>To allow intermediate HTTP nodes (proxy servers, web caches, etc.) to accomplish their functions, some of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_HTTP_header_fields" title="List of HTTP header fields">HTTP headers</a> (found in HTTP requests/responses) are managed <a href="/wiki/Hop-by-hop_transport" title="Hop-by-hop transport">hop-by-hop</a> whereas other HTTP headers are managed <a href="/wiki/End-to-end_principle" title="End-to-end principle">end-to-end</a> (managed only by the source client and by the target web server).
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Web_resource" title="Web resource">web resource</a> is located by a <a href="/wiki/Uniform_resource_locator" class="mw-redirect" title="Uniform resource locator">uniform resource locator</a> (URL), using the <a href="/wiki/Uniform_Resource_Identifier" title="Uniform Resource Identifier">Uniform Resource Identifier</a> (URI) schemes <i>http</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Https" class="mw-redirect" title="Https">https</a></i>. URIs are encoded as <a href="/wiki/Hyperlink" title="Hyperlink">hyperlinks</a> in <a href="/wiki/HTML" title="HTML">HTML</a> documents, so as to form interlinked <a href="/wiki/Hypertext" title="Hypertext">hypertext</a> documents.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc3986&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc3986-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Versions">Versions</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Versions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/CERN_httpd" title="CERN httpd">CERN httpd</a></div>
<p>The protocol has been revised over time. A version is identified as HTTP/# where # is the version number. This article covers aspects of all versions but provides primary coverage for HTTP/0.9, HTTP/1.0, and HTTP/1.1. Separate articles cover <a href="/wiki/HTTP/2" title="HTTP/2">HTTP/2</a> and <a href="/wiki/HTTP/3" title="HTTP/3">HTTP/3</a> in detail.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Version
</th>
<th>Introduced
</th>
<th>Status
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>0.9
</td>
<td>1991
</td>
<td style="background:#FFC7C7;color:black;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Obsolete
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>1.0
</td>
<td>1996
</td>
<td style="background:#FFC7C7;color:black;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Obsolete
</td></tr>
<tr>

</td>

</td>

</td></tr>
<tr>

<td style="background:#9EFF9E;color:black;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Standard
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In HTTP/1.0, a separate TCP <a href="/wiki/Connection-oriented_communication" title="Connection-oriented communication">connection</a> to the same server is made for every resource request.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc1945&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc1945-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §1.3">&#58;&#8202;§1.3&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>In HTTP/1.1, instead a TCP connection can be reused to make multiple resource requests (i.e. of HTML pages, frames, images, <a href="/wiki/Client-side_scripting" class="mw-redirect" title="Client-side scripting">scripts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cascading_Style_Sheets" class="mw-redirect" title="Cascading Style Sheets">stylesheets</a>, etc.).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9112&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9112-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §9.1,9.3">&#58;&#8202;§9.1,9.3&#8202;</span></sup> HTTP/1.1 communications therefore experience less <a href="/wiki/Network_latency" class="mw-redirect" title="Network latency">latency</a> as the establishment of TCP connections presents considerable overhead, especially under high traffic conditions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Enhancements added with HTTP/2 allow for less latency and, in most cases, higher speeds than HTTP/1.1 communications. HTTP/2 adds support for:
</p>
<ul><li>a compressed binary representation of metadata (HTTP headers) instead of a textual one, so that headers require much less space;</li>
<li>a single <a href="/wiki/Internet_Protocol_suite" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet Protocol suite">TCP/IP</a> (usually <a href="/wiki/Encryption" title="Encryption">encrypted</a>) connection per accessed server domain instead of 2 to 8 TCP/IP connections;</li>
<li>one or more bidirectional streams per TCP/IP connection in which HTTP requests and responses are broken down and transmitted in small packets to almost solve the problem of the HOLB (<a href="/wiki/Head-of-line_blocking" title="Head-of-line blocking">head-of-line blocking</a>);<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>a push capability to allow server application to send data to clients whenever new data is available (without forcing clients to request periodically new data to server by using <a href="/wiki/Polling_(computer_science)" title="Polling (computer science)">polling</a> methods).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9113&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9113-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §2">&#58;&#8202;§2&#8202;</span></sup></li></ul>
<p>HTTP/3 uses <a href="/wiki/QUIC" title="QUIC">QUIC</a> + UDP transport protocols instead of TCP. Only the IP layer is used (which UDP, like TCP, builds on). This slightly improves the average speed of communications and avoids the occasional problem of TCP connection <a href="/wiki/TCP_congestion_control" title="TCP congestion control">congestion</a> that can temporarily block or slow down the data flow of all its streams (another form of "<i>head of line blocking</i>").
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Use">Use</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Use"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>HTTP/2 is supported by 71% of websites<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (34.1% HTTP/2 + 36.9% HTTP/3 with backwards compatibility) and supported by almost all web browsers (over 98% of users).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is also supported by major web servers over <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">Transport Layer Security</a> (TLS) using an <a href="/wiki/Application-Layer_Protocol_Negotiation" title="Application-Layer Protocol Negotiation">Application-Layer Protocol Negotiation</a> (ALPN) extension<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc7301&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc7301-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> where <a href="/wiki/TLS_1.2" class="mw-redirect" title="TLS 1.2">TLS 1.2</a> or newer is required.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9113&#95;7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9113-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>HTTP/3 is used on 36.9% of websites<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and is supported by most web browsers, i.e. (at least partially) supported by 97% of users.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> HTTP/3 uses <a href="/wiki/QUIC" title="QUIC">QUIC</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Transmission_Control_Protocol" title="Transmission Control Protocol">TCP</a> for the underlying transport protocol. Like HTTP/2, it does not obsolete previous major versions of the protocol. In 2019, support for HTTP/3 was first added to <a href="/wiki/Cloudflare" title="Cloudflare">Cloudflare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Chrome</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and also enabled in <a href="/wiki/Firefox" title="Firefox">Firefox</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> HTTP/3 has lower latency for real-world web pages and loads faster than HTTP/2, in some cases over three times faster than HTTP/1.1, which is still commonly the only protocol enabled.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/HTTPS" title="HTTPS">HTTPS</a>, the secure variant of HTTP, is used by more than 85% of websites.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Technology">Technology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Technology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Transport_layer">Transport layer</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Transport layer"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>HTTP presumes an underlying and reliable <a href="/wiki/Transport_layer" title="Transport layer">transport layer</a> protocol.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9110&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9110-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §3.3">&#58;&#8202;§3.3&#8202;</span></sup> The standard choice of the underlying protocol prior to HTTP/3 is <a href="/wiki/Transmission_Control_Protocol" title="Transmission Control Protocol">Transmission Control Protocol</a> (TCP). HTTP/3 uses a different transport layer called <a href="/wiki/QUIC" title="QUIC">QUIC</a>, which provides reliability on top of the unreliable <a href="/wiki/User_Datagram_Protocol" title="User Datagram Protocol">User Datagram Protocol</a> (UDP). HTTP/1.1 and earlier have been adapted to be used over plain unreliable UDP in <a href="/wiki/Multicast" title="Multicast">multicast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unicast" title="Unicast">unicast</a> situations, forming HTTPMU and HTTPU. They are used in <a href="/wiki/UPnP" class="mw-redirect" title="UPnP">UPnP</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simple_Service_Discovery_Protocol" title="Simple Service Discovery Protocol">Simple Service Discovery Protocol</a> (SSDP), two protocols usually run on a <a href="/wiki/Local_area_network" title="Local area network">local area network</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Data_exchange">Data exchange</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Data exchange"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>HTTP is a <a href="/wiki/Stateless_protocol" title="Stateless protocol">stateless</a> application-level protocol and it requires a reliable network transport connection to exchange data between client and server.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Connections&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Connections-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In HTTP implementations, <a href="/wiki/Transmission_Control_Protocol" title="Transmission Control Protocol">TCP/IP</a> connections are used using <a href="/wiki/List_of_TCP_and_UDP_port_numbers#Well-known_ports" title="List of TCP and UDP port numbers">well-known ports</a> (typically <a href="/wiki/TCP_port" class="mw-redirect" title="TCP port">port 80</a> if the connection is unencrypted or port 443 if the connection is encrypted, see also <a href="/wiki/List_of_TCP_and_UDP_port_numbers" title="List of TCP and UDP port numbers">List of TCP and UDP port numbers</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9110&#95;19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9110-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §4.2.1,4.2.2">&#58;&#8202;§4.2.1,4.2.2&#8202;</span></sup> In HTTP/2, a TCP/IP connection plus multiple protocol channels are used. In HTTP/3, the application transport protocol <a href="/wiki/QUIC" title="QUIC">QUIC</a> over UDP is used.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Request_and_response_messages_through_connections">Request and response messages through connections</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Request and response messages through connections"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Data is exchanged through a sequence of <a href="/wiki/Request-response" class="mw-redirect" title="Request-response">request–response messages</a> which are exchanged by a <a href="/wiki/Session_layer" title="Session layer">session layer</a> transport connection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Connections&#95;20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Connections-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An HTTP client initially tries to establish a connection, real or virtual, with a server. An HTTP server listening on the port accepts the connection and then waits for a client's request message. The client sends its HTTP request message. Upon receiving the request the server sends back an HTTP response message, which includes header(s) plus a body if it is required. The body of this response message is typically the requested resource, although an error message or other information may also be returned. At any time and for many reasons, either the client or server can close the connection. Closing a connection is usually advertised by one or more HTTP headers in the last request or response.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9112&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9112-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §9.1">&#58;&#8202;§9.1&#8202;</span></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Persistent_connections">Persistent connections</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Persistent connections"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/HTTP_persistent_connection" title="HTTP persistent connection">HTTP persistent connection</a></div>
<p>In HTTP/0.9, the TCP/IP connection is always closed after server response has been sent, so it is never persistent.
</p><p>In HTTP/1.0, the TCP/IP connection should always be closed by server after a response has been sent.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc1945&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc1945-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In HTTP/1.1, a keep-alive-mechanism was officially introduced so that a connection could be reused for more than one request/response. Such persistent connections reduce request <a href="/wiki/Network_latency" class="mw-redirect" title="Network latency">latency</a> perceptibly because the client does not need to re-negotiate the <a href="/wiki/Transmission_Control_Protocol#Connection_establishment" title="Transmission Control Protocol">TCP 3-Way-Handshake connection</a> after the first request has been sent. Another positive side effect is that, in general, the connection becomes faster with time due to TCP's <a href="/wiki/TCP_congestion_control#Slow_start" title="TCP congestion control">slow-start</a>-mechanism.
</p><p>HTTP/1.1 added also <a href="/wiki/HTTP_pipelining" title="HTTP pipelining">HTTP pipelining</a> in order to further reduce lag time when using persistent connections by allowing clients to send multiple requests before waiting for each response.  This optimization was never considered really safe because a few web servers and many <a href="/wiki/Proxy_server" title="Proxy server">proxy servers</a>, specially transparent proxy servers placed in Internet / <a href="/wiki/Intranet" title="Intranet">Intranets</a> between clients and servers, did not handle pipelined requests properly (they served only the first request discarding the others, they closed the connection because they saw more data after the first request or some proxies even returned responses out of order etc.).  Because of this, only HEAD and some GET requests (i.e. limited to real file requests and so with <a href="/wiki/URL" title="URL">URLs</a> without query string used as a command, etc.) could be pipelined in a <a href="#Safe_method">safe</a> and <a href="#Idempotent_method">idempotent</a> mode.  After many years of struggling with the problems introduced by enabling pipelining, this feature was first disabled and then removed from most browsers also because of the announced adoption of HTTP/2.
</p><p>HTTP/2 extended the usage of persistent connections by multiplexing many concurrent requests/responses through a single TCP/IP connection.
</p><p>HTTP/3 does not use TCP/IP connections but QUIC + UDP.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Content_retrieval_optimizations">Content retrieval optimizations</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Content retrieval optimizations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In HTTP/0.9, a requested resource was always sent in its entirety.
</p><p>HTTP/1.0 added headers to manage resources cached by a client in order to allow conditional GET requests. 
</p>
<ul><li>A server must return the entire content of the requested resource only if its last modified time is not known by the client or if it changed since the last full response to a GET request.</li></ul>
<ul><li>Header <code class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-text mw-content-ltr" style="" dir="ltr">Content-Encoding</code> was added to specify whether the returned content is <a href="/wiki/HTTP_compression" title="HTTP compression">compressed</a>.</li></ul>
<ul><li>If the size of the content is not known in advance (i.e. because it is dynamically generated) then the header <code>Content-Length</code> would <i>not</i> be included. The client would assume that transfer was complete when the connection closed, but a premature close would leave the client with partial content yet the client would not know it's partial.</li></ul>
<p>HTTP/1.1 introduced and later versions provide:
</p>
<ul><li>Headers to better manage the conditional retrieval of cached resources.</li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chunked_transfer_encoding" title="Chunked transfer encoding">Chunked transfer encoding</a> allows content to be streamed in chunks in order to reliably send it even when the server does not know its length in advance (i.e. because it is dynamically generated, etc.).</li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Byte_serving" title="Byte serving">Byte range serving</a> allows a client to request portions (ranges of bytes) of a resource. This is useful to resume an interrupted download (when a file is very large), when only a part of a content has to be shown or dynamically added to the already visible part by a browser (i.e. only the first or the following n comments of a web page) in order to spare time, bandwidth and system resources, etc.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Application_session">Application session <span class="anchor" id="HTTP&#95;session"></span></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Application session"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>As a <a href="/wiki/Stateless_protocol" title="Stateless protocol">stateless protocol</a>, HTTP does not require the web server to retain information or status about each user for the duration of multiple requests. If a <a href="/wiki/Web_application" title="Web application">web application</a> needs an <a href="/wiki/Session_(computer_science)" title="Session (computer science)">application session</a>, it implements it via <a href="/wiki/HTTP_cookie" title="HTTP cookie">HTTP cookies</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> hidden <a href="/wiki/Variable_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Variable (computer science)">variables</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Form_(web)" class="mw-redirect" title="Form (web)">web form</a> or another mechanism.
</p><p>Typically, to start a session, an interactive <a href="/wiki/Login" title="Login">login</a> is performed, and to end a session, a <a href="/wiki/Logout" class="mw-redirect" title="Logout">logout</a> is requested by the user. These kind of operations use a custom <a href="/wiki/Authentication" title="Authentication">authentication</a> mechanism, not <a href="#Authentication">HTTP authentication</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Authentication">Authentication</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Authentication"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>HTTP provides multiple authentication schemes such as <a href="/wiki/Basic_access_authentication" title="Basic access authentication">basic access authentication</a> and <a href="/wiki/Digest_access_authentication" title="Digest access authentication">digest access authentication</a> which operate via a challenge–response mechanism whereby the server identifies and issues a challenge before serving the requested content.
</p><p>HTTP provides a general framework for access control and authentication, via an extensible set of challenge–response authentication schemes, which can be used by a server to challenge a client request and by a client to provide authentication information.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9110&#95;19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9110-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The authentication mechanisms described above belong to the HTTP protocol and are managed by client and server HTTP software (if configured to require authentication before allowing client access to one or more web resources), and not by the web applications using an <a href="#Application_session">application session</a>.
</p><p>The HTTP authentication specification includes realms that provide an arbitrary, implementation-specific construct for further dividing resources common to a given root <a href="/wiki/Uniform_Resource_Identifier" title="Uniform Resource Identifier">URI</a>. The realm value string, if present, is combined with the canonical root URI to form the protection space component of the challenge. This in effect allows the server to define separate authentication scopes under one root URI.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Internet&#95;Engineering&#95;Task&#95;Force-2022&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Internet_Engineering_Task_Force-2022-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Encrypted_connection">Encrypted connection</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Encrypted connection"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The most popular way of establishing an encrypted HTTP connection is <a href="/wiki/HTTPS" title="HTTPS">HTTPS</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Two other methods for establishing an encrypted HTTP connection also exist: <a href="/wiki/Secure_Hypertext_Transfer_Protocol" title="Secure Hypertext Transfer Protocol">Secure Hypertext Transfer Protocol</a>, and using the <a href="/wiki/HTTP/1.1_Upgrade_header" title="HTTP/1.1 Upgrade header">HTTP/1.1 Upgrade header</a> to specify an upgrade to TLS. Browser support for these two is, however, nearly non-existent.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Message_format">Message format<span class="anchor" id="message-format"></span></h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Message format"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Http_request_telnet_ubuntu.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Http_request_telnet_ubuntu.png/250px-Http_request_telnet_ubuntu.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="186" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Http_request_telnet_ubuntu.png/500px-Http_request_telnet_ubuntu.png 1.5x" data-file-width="1232" data-file-height="918" /></a><figcaption>An HTTP/1.1 request made using telnet. The parts of the transaction are shown in different colors: request in red, response header in purple, and response body in green.</figcaption></figure>
<p>This section describes messages for HTTP/1.1. Later versions, <a href="/wiki/HTTP/2" title="HTTP/2">HTTP/2</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/HTTP/3" title="HTTP/3">HTTP/3</a>, use a <a href="/wiki/Communication_protocol#Binary" title="Communication protocol">binary protocol</a>, where headers are encoded in a single <code>HEADERS</code> and zero or more <code>CONTINUATION</code> frames using HPACK<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (HTTP/2) or QPACK (HTTP/3), which both provide efficient header compression. The request or response line from HTTP/1 has also been replaced by several pseudo-header fields, each beginning with a colon (<code>:</code>).
</p><p>At the highest level, a message consists of a header followed by a body.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Header">Header<span class="anchor" id="message-header"></span></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Header"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A header consists of lines of <a href="/wiki/ASCII" title="ASCII">ASCII</a> <a href="/wiki/Plain_text" title="Plain text">text</a>; each terminated with a <a href="/wiki/Carriage_return" title="Carriage return">carriage return</a> and <a href="/wiki/Line_feed" class="mw-redirect" title="Line feed">line feed</a> sequence. The layout for both a request and a response header is:
</p>
<dl><dt>Start line</dt>
<dd>Structured data that differs for request vs. response.</dd>
<dt>Header fields</dt>
<dd>Zero or more <a href="/wiki/HTTP_request_header_field" class="mw-redirect" title="HTTP request header field">header field</a> lines (at least 1 for HTTP/1.1); see below.</dd>
<dt>Empty line</dt>
<dd>Marks the end of the header.</dd></dl>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Body">Body<span class="anchor" id="message-body"></span></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Body"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A body consists of data in any format; not limited to ASCII. The format must match that specified by the <code class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-text mw-content-ltr" style="" dir="ltr">Content-Type</code> header field if the message contains one. A body is optional or, in other words, can be blank.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Entity">Entity<span class="anchor" id="message-entity"></span></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Entity"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Before HTTP/2, the term <i>entity</i> was used to mean the body plus header fields that describe the body. In particular, not all headers were considered part of the entity. The term <i>entity header</i> referred to a header that was considered part of the entity, and sometimes the body was called the <i>entity body</i>. Modern documentation uses <i>body</i> and <i>header</i> without using <i>entity</i>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Header_field">Header field<span class="anchor" id="message-header-field"></span></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Header field"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_HTTP_header_fields" title="List of HTTP header fields">List of HTTP header fields</a></div>
<p>A header field represents <a href="/wiki/Metadata" title="Metadata">metadata</a> about its containing message such as how the body is encoded (via <a href="/wiki/HTTP_compression#Content-Encoding_tokens" title="HTTP compression">Content-Encoding</a>), the session verification and identification of the client (as in <a href="/wiki/Browser_cookies" class="mw-redirect" title="Browser cookies">browser cookies</a>, IP address, <a href="/wiki/User-agent" class="mw-redirect" title="User-agent">user-agent</a>) or their anonymity thereof (VPN or proxy masking, user-agent spoofing), how the server should handle data (as in <a href="/wiki/Do-Not-Track" class="mw-redirect" title="Do-Not-Track">Do-Not-Track</a> or <a href="/wiki/Global_Privacy_Control" title="Global Privacy Control">Global Privacy Control</a>), the age (the time it has resided in a shared <a href="/wiki/Web_cache" title="Web cache">cache</a>) of the document being downloaded, and much more. Generally, the information of a header field is used by <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a> and not shown to the <a href="/wiki/User_(computing)" title="User (computing)">user</a>.
</p><p>A header field line is formatted as a <a href="/wiki/Name-value_pair" class="mw-redirect" title="Name-value pair">name-value pair</a> with a colon separator. <a href="/wiki/Whitespace_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Whitespace (computer science)">Whitespace</a> is not allowed around the name, but leading and trailing whitespace is ignored for the value part. Unlike a method name that must match exactly (case-sensitive),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> a header field name is matched ignoring case although often shown with each word capitalized.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, the following are header fields for <code class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-text mw-content-ltr" style="" dir="ltr">Host</code> and <code class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-text mw-content-ltr" style="" dir="ltr">Accept-Language</code>.
</p>
<pre>Host: www.example.com
Accept-Language: en
</pre>
<p>The standards do not limit the size of a header field or the number of fields in a message. However, most servers, clients, and proxy software impose limits for practical and security reasons. For example, the Apache 2.3 server by default limits the size of each field to 8190 bytes, and there can be at most 100 header fields in a single request.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Although deprecated by RFC 7230,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in the past, long lines could be split into multiple lines with a continuation line starting with a <a href="/wiki/Space_(punctuation)" title="Space (punctuation)">space</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tab_character" title="Tab character">tab</a> character.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Request">Request<span class="anchor" id="Request&#95;message"></span></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Request"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A request is sent by a client to a server. The start line includes a method name, a request URI and the protocol version with a single space between each field.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The following request start line specifies method <code class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-text mw-content-ltr" style="" dir="ltr">GET</code>, URI <code class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-text mw-content-ltr" style="" dir="ltr">/customer/123</code> and protocol version <code class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-text mw-content-ltr" style="" dir="ltr">HTTP/1.1</code>:
</p>
<pre>GET /customer/123 HTTP/1.1
</pre>
<p><a href="/wiki/List_of_HTTP_header_fields#Request_fields" title="List of HTTP header fields">Request header fields</a> allow the client to pass additional information beyond the request line, acting as request modifiers (similarly to the parameters of a procedure). They give information about the client, about the target resource, or about the expected handling of the request. In the HTTP/1.1 protocol, all header fields except <code>Host</code> are optional.
</p><p>A request line containing only the path name is accepted by servers to maintain compatibility with HTTP clients before the HTTP/1.0 specification in <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><a href="/wiki/RFC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="RFC (identifier)">RFC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc1945">1945</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Resource">Resource<span class="anchor" id="request-resource"></span></h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Resource"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The protocol structures transaction as operating on resources. What a resource represents, whether pre-existing data or data that is generated dynamically, depends on the implementation of the server. Often, the resource corresponds to a file or the output of an executable running on the server.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Method">Method<span class="anchor" id="request-method"></span><span class="anchor" id="Request&#95;methods"></span></h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Method"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A request identifies a method (sometimes informally called <i>verb</i>) to classify the desired action to be performed on a resource. The HTTP/1.0 specification<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc1945&#95;3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc1945-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §8">&#58;&#8202;§8&#8202;</span></sup> defined the GET, HEAD, and POST methods as well as listing the PUT, DELETE, LINK and UNLINK methods under additional methods. However, the HTTP/1.1 specification<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc2616&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc2616-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §9">&#58;&#8202;§9&#8202;</span></sup> added five new methods: PUT, DELETE, CONNECT, OPTIONS, and TRACE. Any client can use any method and the server can be configured to support any combination of methods. If a method is unknown to an intermediate, it will be treated as an unsafe and <a href="/wiki/Idempotence" title="Idempotence">non-idempotent</a> method. There is no limit to the number of methods that can be defined, which allows for future methods to be specified without breaking existing infrastructure. For example, <a href="/wiki/WebDAV" title="WebDAV">WebDAV</a> defined seven new methods and <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><a href="/wiki/RFC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="RFC (identifier)">RFC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5789">5789</a> specified the <a href="/wiki/PATCH_(HTTP)" title="PATCH (HTTP)">PATCH</a> method. A general-purpose web server is required to implement at least GET and HEAD, and all other methods are considered optional by the specification.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9110&#95;19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9110-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §9.1">&#58;&#8202;§9.1&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>Method names are case sensitive.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9112&#95;4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9112-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §3">&#58;&#8202;§3&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9110&#95;19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9110-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §9.1">&#58;&#8202;§9.1&#8202;</span></sup> This is in contrast to HTTP header field names which are case-insensitive.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9110&#95;19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9110-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §6.3">&#58;&#8202;§6.3&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="GET&#95;method"></span>
</p>
<dl><dt>GET</dt>
<dd>The request is for a representation of a resource. The server should only <a href="/wiki/Data_retrieval" title="Data retrieval">retrieve data</a>; not modify state.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Internet&#95;Engineering&#95;Task&#95;Force-2022&#95;1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Internet_Engineering_Task_Force-2022-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For retrieving without making changes, GET is preferred over POST, as it can be <a href="/wiki/Addressability" title="Addressability">addressed</a> through a <a href="/wiki/URL" title="URL">URL</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (November 2025)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> This enables bookmarking and sharing and makes GET responses eligible for <a href="/wiki/Browser_cache" class="mw-redirect" title="Browser cache">caching</a>, which can save bandwidth. The <a href="/wiki/W3C" class="mw-redirect" title="W3C">W3C</a> has published guidance principles on this distinction, saying, "<a href="/wiki/Web_application" title="Web application">Web application</a> design should be informed by the above principles, but also by the relevant limitations."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></dd></dl>
<p><span class="anchor" id="HEAD&#95;method"></span>
</p>
<dl><dt>HEAD</dt>
<dd>The request is like a GET except that the response should <i>not</i> include the representation data in the body. This is useful for retrieving the representation <a href="/wiki/Metadata" title="Metadata">metadata</a> in the response header, without having to transfer the entire representation. Uses include checking whether a page is available via the status code and getting the size of a <a href="/wiki/Computer_file" title="Computer file">file</a> via header field <code>Content-Length</code>.</dd></dl>
<p><span class="anchor" id="POST&#95;method"></span>
</p>
<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/POST_(HTTP)" title="POST (HTTP)">POST</a></dt>
<dd>The request is to process a resource in some way. For example, it is used for posting a message to an <a href="/wiki/Internet_forum" title="Internet forum">Internet forum</a>, subscribing to a <a href="/wiki/Mailing_list" title="Mailing list">mailing list</a>, or completing an <a href="/wiki/Online_shopping" title="Online shopping">online shopping</a> transaction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9110&#95;19-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9110-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §9.3.3">&#58;&#8202;§9.3.3&#8202;</span></sup></dd></dl>
<p><span class="anchor" id="PUT&#95;method"></span>
</p>
<dl><dt>PUT</dt>
<dd>The request is to create or update a resource with the state in the request. A distinction from POST is that the client specifies the target location on the server.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9110&#95;19-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9110-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §9.3.4">&#58;&#8202;§9.3.4&#8202;</span></sup></dd></dl>
<p><span class="anchor" id="DELETE&#95;method"></span>
</p>
<dl><dt>DELETE</dt>
<dd>The request is to delete a resource.</dd></dl>
<p><span class="anchor" id="CONNECT&#95;method"></span>
</p>
<dl><dt>CONNECT</dt>
<dd>Requests that the intermediary establish a <a href="/wiki/Tunneling_protocol" title="Tunneling protocol">TCP/IP tunnel</a> to the origin server identified by the request target. It is often used to secure connections through one or more <a href="/wiki/HTTP_proxy" class="mw-redirect" title="HTTP proxy">HTTP proxies</a> with <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">TLS</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9110&#95;19-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9110-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: §9.3.6">&#58;&#8202;§9.3.6&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> See <a href="/wiki/HTTP_tunnel#HTTP_CONNECT_method" title="HTTP tunnel">HTTP CONNECT method</a>.</dd></dl>
<p><span class="anchor" id="OPTIONS&#95;method"></span>
</p>
<dl><dt>OPTIONS</dt>
<dd>Request is for a report of the HTTP methods that are supported for a resource. This can be used to check the functionality of a web server by requesting '*' instead of a specific resource.</dd></dl>
<p><span class="anchor" id="TRACE&#95;method"></span>
</p>
<dl><dt>TRACE</dt>
<dd>Requests the server to respond with the received request in the response body. That way a client can see what (if any) changes or additions have been made by intermediaries. Useful for debugging.</dd></dl>
<p><span class="anchor" id="PATCH&#95;method"></span>
</p>
<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/PATCH_(HTTP)" title="PATCH (HTTP)">PATCH</a></dt>
<dd>The request is to modify a resource according to its partial state in the request. Compared to PUT, this can save bandwidth by sending only part of a resource's representation instead of all of it.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5789&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5789-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></dd></dl>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Properties&#95;of&#95;request&#95;methods&#95;table"></span>
</p>
